===== PAGE 6 =====
“HAYOM HARAT OLAM -
TODAY IS THE BIRTHDAY OF THE WORLD”
“Hayom Harat Olam” is a prayer we say following the blowing of the Shofar on Rosh Hashanah. It confirms
the Jewish tradition that the New Year is celebrated as the birthday of the world and as the beginning of human
existence. More important, because it is the Jewish celebration of Rosh Hashanah and not the rather shallow
observance of the secular New Year, it should give us pause for reflection.
With the passage of time, the New Year, like all birthdays, becomes a subtle blend of happiness and sobriety.
We enjoy the New Year cards of well wishers but we realize that we are a year older and hence closer to the end of
life. We become acutely aware of the passage of time.
It is of course inevitable that we should look back upon the past—not only last year, but the whole of life, and
recognize that while we have achieved much, there is a great deal which remains unfulfilled in our life. We hope and
plan and dream but much of it remains unrealized. The overriding themes of the Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur
I season are intended to be helpful. A sense of “ A1 het” - “for the sins we have sinned”, the sense of remorse, for the
mistakes that we have made, the seeking of atonement, the promise of “Teshuvah” -the hope for change is
I fundamental to our psychological and emotional well being. No year passes without some defeat, without some
! sense of frustration and without some sense of “what might have been”. The New Year reminds us of the
¡ opportunities of renewal and beginning again.
There is no more imperious tyrant than a calendar. It is the outward reminder that we are all creatures of time.
A clock is the closest ally of the calendar. Unlike sports and athletic events we cannot “stop the clock” of life.
Worse, there is never any “replay”. The thought of our powerlessness might well paralyze us into inactivity were it
not for the holiday themes of the Mahsor, our holiday prayerbook that forces us to look elsewhere. The notion of
“Teshuvah” — that we can always return to God’s presence, to sensing His nearness by realizing the best within
ourselves is comforting. There are other ways of celebrating New Year birthdays. Perhaps it is in our awareness
that life is measured not only in days, but in deeds...that life is measured in growth. What is alive-grows. What
grows is alive. Time will take care of our getting older. We must look after our getting wiser. We are summoned on
Rosh Hashanah to resolve that the New Year will make us more understanding, more sympathetic, more deeply
touched by the affliction of others. We need also be more appreciative of the beauty around us and of the gift of life
itself. People who use the Siddur sensitively—people who truly worship are aware of this.
Unless we are prepared to accept the changes which time brings, to be reminded of the calendar, we cannot
live sensible lives. We tear off the page of the calendar after a week or month has passed. Y ester day is gone, today
is fleeting, tomorrow is always on the way. Every moment offers us an opportunity for learning, for expanding our
knowledge. The challenge of the Torah way is described in the words “and you shall meditate on it day and night.”
Every moment should be an opportunity for making the life of some individual happier and more fulfilling. Time is
suffused with the sense of eternity. The Shabbat is such a time. So is Kol Nidre. Only those who can experience
these hours when we forget time can be said to be truly alive. Living...true living, is the ability to see the enduring in
the swiftly, vanishing moment of time.
When we rise to say “Hayom harat olam” on Rosh Hashanah let us remember the true challenge of the year to
come...the true challenge of life.
The above thoughts were presented by the Portnoys with the hope that it will nurture some reflection on our
most precious possession—the gift of life.
EAST COAST FRUIT CO.
Walter Portnoy - Mrs. Louis Portnoy and Families

I wish to thank the following people who make this yearbook possible -- Esther
Fishier who works so diligently getting ads and who makes this book a major fund-raiser;
to Florence Robin for her valuable advice; to Irvin Ghelerter, who oversees the printing
at Mendelsons; to Blanche Slott for sharing her talents on the cover and title page
designs; to Don Morris, photographer, who donates his time and equipment: and to you
who advertise, participate and share in this fabulous synagogue-center ... I say “the best
is yet to come, be there.”
My family and I wish all of you a very happy New Year.
Isabel Balotin

===== PAGE 16 =====
Rabbi & Mrs. David Gaffney
I write this message because I must -- it is an epilogue for two weeks of summer travel
that forever will be etched in my memory.
Inge and I, along with some rabbis, Jewish communal leaders, and scholars of Eastern
European affairs, traveled to Hungary and Rumania this summer. Our intention was to
visit with Jews, their leaders, their synagogues and community institutions. We returned
with a renewed appreciation of the freedoms we enjoy in this glorious land in which we
live, coupled with the sense, of what I insist must be, the awesome reponsibility that
American Jews share.
The Rabbis of the Talmud, in a statement intended to assure their anxious and self
doubting contemporaries that Israel is an eternal people, taught that on the day the great
Rabbi Akiba died, Rabbi Judah, author of the Mishna, was born. When one generation’s
intellectual and spiritual leaders are replaced by others, the eternity of people is made
possible. Jewish historians have expressed similar sentiments about the great historical
Jewish communities of the past. They point out that the decline of Palestinian Jewry
which followed the destruction of the Temple saw the emerging ascendency of the great
communities of Babylonian Jewry, making possible the continuity of the rabbinic tradition which emerged first in Palestine.
When Babylonia’s demise was at hand, following years of oppressive Moslem rule, communities of Babylonian Jewry, making
possible the continuity of the rabbinic tradition which emerged first in Palestine. When Babylonia’s demise was at hand, following
years of oppressive Moslem rule, history witnessed the rise of the great Spanish Jewish community along with its philosophers,
poets and teachers.
These thoughts emerged into consciousness by our visit to Rumania. In community after community we saw Jewish “ghost
towns” of what were once great centers of Jewish learning, worship, piety and Hassidic fervor. Imposing synagogue structures
were being used as factories and warehouses and others, with their broken windowpanes and gathering dust served as faint
reminders of what once was. Rumanian Jewry which once numbered 800,000 souls now numbers 15,000. Half of the former
population was decimated by the Nazi hordes and most of the abundance of people fled to Israel. Despite the herculean efforts of
the charismatic personality of Rumania’s Chief Rabbi Rosen, whom I had the pleasure of meeting, Rumanian Jewry is virtually, as
one person put it, an old age home. The few small gatherings of a minyan that I encountered in a number of synagogues, when
asked, could not recall the last Bar Mitzvah they celebrated and the last time they were privileged to attend a Brit.
The conclusions drawn from this experience should be obvious. That communities are created, that they thrive and
sometimes disappear as a result of external circumstances is, historically speaking, in the nature of things. But that they might be
replaced by worthy successors, by peoples and communities that proclaim the same verities, the same principles of faith, of love
of God, of love of man, of a fidelity to our traditions and to the world in which we live - that is made possible by the sheer exercise
of the will and of the determination of a people committed to the eternity of Israel. A glorious future remains only a hope unless
people make it so. When our prayerbook states that “generation after generation will sing Thy praise”, it is not stated as a
guaranty but as a hope.
Our visit to these countries underscored what I had always known and what I feel I must convey to you; namely, that there is a
one-to-one relationship between the vitality of a synagogue and those institutions which speak to its spirit, and the vitality of a
community. We cannot exist as a proud self-conscious collection of Jews unless we recognize that certain institutions, i.e. the
synagogue, speak to the soul of what we are; to our very essence. A community cannot die, given the stability of external forces,
if the synagogue and its house of study continue to be a vital center of its people’s life. In that regard each and every Jew who
turns to the synagogue for its spiritual nourishment, for meaning and purpose in his or her life, becomes part of the hope and
promise of our history.
The High Holiday season is upon us. We will recite the words “Zochreinu L’haim” - “Remember us for life.” I am haunted by
the image, not only of a former great community of Jews in Rumania, but by all the great communities of our past, pleading,
“Remember us for life.” This prayer for life is not only
stated for our personal benefit, our individual life with its
too often preoccupation with self concerns, but also for
our collective lives. We can assure the survival of Jews
only when we have committed ourselves to the
meaningful and purposeful living inherent in Judaism.
I wish I could commit our entire congregation to
become a Jewish “sister city” to the central Rumanian
city of Tigur Morush. Its stately and beautiful synagogue
that used to serve as the center of worship for thousands
of Jews barely has a minyan on Shabbat. Tigur Morush
may be no more. We might, however, dedicate ourselves
to take its place, hence assuring not only the survival of
the principles and ideals for which those people lived, but
also to assure the growth and continued flourishing of a
people destined to live forever. This is our responsibility.
This is also our glory.
On this New Year, Inge and I and our entire family join
in wishing you and yours a year of health and happiness,
contentment and peace.
Rabbi David Gaffney
Asher Gaffney
Shalva & Mickey Schlisser

Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held daily, morning and
evening in the Witten Chapel. Friday Family services, Sabbath and Holiday
Services are observed throughout the entire year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish customs are observed
in connection with Circumcision, Naming of Girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar
Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah, Confirmation, Marriages, Conversion to Judaism,
Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery & Day Care with outstanding
program of secular and religious content. Hebrew school, carefully graded
with intensive Hebrew content, including Jewish history and customs,
Sunday School for kindergarten, first and second grades: Solomon Schechter
Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew School, 1st through 7th grades;
Hebrew High School with Pre-Jr. and Junior Congregations and Minyanaire.
Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of juvenile and adult
Jewish books; Adult Education, religious and cultural Jewish courses; music
programs of religious and cultural Jewish music; P.T.A.; Sisterhood,
Ha’Rayim, a fellowship of young families; Center Couples Club, social
activities provided by Mr. and Mrs. Club, Single Adults; Family Recreation
Program (Olympic pool), summer week-days and Sundays for all ages.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United Synagogue Youth,
various age levels are served by Center Youth League, Charles Palot Club,
Kadima, Chalutzim, Boy Scouts, Cub Scouts, Girl Scouts, Brownies; Sunday
Funday; athletics, programs for youth and adults; Center Day Camp and
summer program at our 36 acre site on San Jose Blvd.
Family Recreation Program — Center Campsite . . . swimming, athletics,
snack bar; available for members during the summer months. Campsite
includes olympic-size swimming pool, 2 tennis courts, baseball, soccer and
football fields and playground.
For additional information, call the Center office — 268-6736

===== PAGE 20 =====
Message from the Administrative Director
To me, the Center has become a way of life — not because I have my daily
duties as Administrative Director, but because the position evolved from the
many years of volunteer work that started with membership in the Sisterhood,
leadership in all phases of the Girl and Boy Scout programs, advisor to all ages of
Center youth, Chairman of the Youth Commission, Sisterhood President,
member of the Center Board of Directors and finally Day Camp Director.
I mention all of these not in a boastful way, but with the hope that those of
our membership who are not involved in the multi-dynamic programs of our
Synagogue-Center will know that they are missing many inspiring and long
remembered experiences. There is more to be gained in “Nachas” and rewards
than the time and effort given.
As Administrative Director, I am here to maintain our building, oversee the
food program and to serve our members in all areas, to encourage your
volunteer involvement, assist you with organizational, Bar/Bat Mitzvah and
wedding plans — or whatever the need may be.
To all our members. I extend an open invitation for you to call me anytime.
My husband, Louis, who is my silent partner, right hand and sounding
board when the going gets rough, joins me in wishing our entire congregation a
joyous and rewarding New Year.
BLANCHE SLOTT
Administrative Director
Blanche Slott
Louis and Blanche Slott

Message From The President
NATHAN KRESTUL
Dear Friends,
As I look back on my first year as your president, there is much
satisfaction in my recollections.
Our programs and activities have continued to progress. Some
notable areas of which we can take pride are our religious, educational and
service activities.
With the help of our dedicated staff led by Rabbi Gaffney and our
hard working lay leadership we have funded our largest-ever operating
budget. Our schools have broadened their age spread by adding new
programs for two year olds and by establishing a new seventh grade. With
new programs and returning pupils our schools will be at a record
enrollment level. Our congregation should note and be proud of the
continuing scholastic success of many of our school alumni.
The enthusiastic participation of our Minyan captains and their “regulars” on a weekly basis gives us a solid
backbone for our religious programs. Our Adult Education, Hebrew Literacy, Library, Sisterhood and Minyanaire
programs have all added to the ambiance of our congregation. The social and special events activities of our
congregation have gotten city wide recognition for our Center.
For the coming year we need to continue our strong efforts and to work to strengthen our weaker areas. In
order to do this, we need the cooperation of all of our members. One good way to accomplish our goals is by using
volunteer effort. If all of our membership will give us a small amount of time and effort in one of many activities or
committees, it will enable the congregation to spread the work load and to service all areas of need. To do all the
jobs we want to cover we need more manpower. Please make your interests and availability known to the Center
office so that the administration can work to solve some more of our problems.
It is with much satisfaction and some frustration that we enter the second year of my administration. If you,
the congregation, will continue your support our efforts will be rewarded with success.
In closing, I wish to thank my wife Carolyn for her patience and help while I am on loan to the congregation.
Le Shana Tovah Tikatevu,
Nathan Krestul, President
The Krestuls: Charles, Miriam, Nate, Carolyn and JoAnne

Simhat Torah Honorces
Ralph Mizrahi - Hat an Torah
This year Ralph Mizrahi wFl re¬
ceive the honor of Ha tan Torah
(Bridegroom of the Torah). Ra]ph
has served the Center in many
ways. As a registered Architect he
became active as Chairman of the
Architects Committee of the Center
which laid plans for the Activities
Building on Third and Silver
Streets. With foresight and deter¬
mination, Ralph and members of
the Executive and Building Fund
Committees, were responsible for
building the School wing on Third
and Silver.
His commitment to Jewish values
is demonstrated by his active parti¬
cipation in Shabbat and Holiday
observances. He takes great pride
that his family has continued to be
active1 in the Synagogue for three
generations. His son Charles has
served as President of the Center
and son David is an active member
of the Board of Directors. His
grandchildren attend the Center
schools and participate in all the
activities. His daughter Deborah is
involved with her Synagogue in
Framingham where she serves as
bookkeeper. His wife, Evelyn, past
Sisterhood President, has been sup¬
portive and just as active in her
own right.
Ralph remains very modest about
all he has done for the Center.
During his presidency, 1956^58, he
wrote how important it is to per¬
petuate Judaism. “An attitude must
be developed toward giving both
morally and financially—so that it
continuously flows, nurtures and
maintains in the hearts of our chil¬
dren the strength and vitality of
our religious and cultural heritage.”
Ralph personifies the leadership
that has resulted in the many ac¬
complishments of the Center. All of
us, old and young, are grateful to
Ralph Mizrahi for his dedication
and generosity to the Jewish Center.
E. Alan Gordon - Hatan Bereshit
Selected to receive the honor of
the Hatan Mereshit (Bridegroom
of the Beginning) is E. Alan Gor¬
don. Alan’s career at the Center
began at the tender age of four
when he entered Kindergarten. He
was educated in our religious
schools and became an active mem¬
ber of U.S.Y.
He grew up in a home where
concern for the synagogue was of
the utmost importance. His father,
the late Robert J. Gordon, served
as Chairman of the Board and
Treasurer, the latter being the
position Alan held before becoming
Vice-President of Finances.
He has been a top solicitor for
the Building Fund and has dili¬
gently collected accounts receivable
for the General Fund. Always good-
natured and ready to serve, Alan
has raised thousands of dollars for
the car raffles. His quick wit has
made him one of our “top sales¬
men.” Community-wise, Alan has
been a Board member of the Jew¬
ish Federation.
Beneath all the jokes Alan is a
very serious and dedicated young
man, not only to his synagogue but
to his wife, Rene’e who is President
of the Sisterhood, and to his five
children, Dana, Robert, Michael,
Ronnie and Faye, and his mother,
Lil Gordon.
Sybil Ansbacher - Woman of Valor
The “Woman of Valor” honor
will be bestowed on Sybil Ansbach-
er during Simhat Torah Services.
Twenty-two years ago, Sybil came
to( Jacksonville as the new bride of
Lukie Ansbacher. Raised as a Re¬
formed Jew in Miami, Sybil was
anxious to learn more about Con¬
servative Judaism.
The first organization she joined
was the Center Sisterhood. After
serving on various committees she
became Cultural Vice-President and
then Sisterhood President. The So¬
cial Action committee was one of
the innovations bom of Sybil’s
presidency. Preceding Sisterhood
president, Blanche Slott and Sybil’s
Fund Raising Vice President and
close friend, Sylvia Shorstein, both
describe her as a “joy to work with.”
When her sons, Richard, Law¬
rence and Barry, were in school,
Sybil volunteered as a teacher’s
aide in the Day School and was
involved in many school activities.
She was an inspiring Kadima ad¬
visor for two years and a Den
Mother for Cub Scouts.
Involvement in the Center is a
family affair. Her husband, Lukie,
has been on the Executive Commit¬
tee for many years. Her interest
in youth activities inspired her
boys to become active in the Center
Youth programs where they have
held positions of leadership.
She is a past President of N.C.-
J.W., and now serves as Area 17
Chairwoman, leadership trainer and
trouble shooter. Civic-minded and
concerned for people, she has been
motivated to become active in many
civic organizations such as United
Way, Mental Health Association,
City HUD, and NCCJ, and more.
Sybil cares for her people, her
family and the world around her
and she strives to make it a better
place for alL

Daily /I\ii?yai)
The Jacksonville Jewish Center take pride in its Daily Minyan.
Through the tireless efforts of the captains and co-captains, we
have minyans every morning and evening.
The anniversary date of the passing of a loved one presents an
opportunity to observe a Mitzvah, if not a double Mitzvah.
The Mitzvah of observing the Yahrzeit is obvious - the second
Mitzvah is the opportunity to insure a minyan so that others,
besides yourself, can also observe or attend a service for their own
reasons.
The Daily Minyan needs you. For a satisfying and rewarding
experience, attend once or twice a month on a regular basis.
If you plan to attend, call the captain of the day and time.

CENTER SISTERHOOD
As our theme, “Therefore Choose Life”, guides us for a
second year, we continue to strive for new ways to make more
knowledgeable choices. The theme for the coming year
provides guidance for wise choices to make our personal lives
richer and more meaningful. When we, as individuals, choose
to learn, we are taking a significant step toward choosing life.
The word “choice” reflects the current mood among women
today. Never before have women had so many choices open to
them and never before have women had to be so serious and
resolute in their decision making. We are aware of the influence
a woman has upon her family. We know that many women
have chosen to work outside the home, sometimes not by choice but of necessity. At
the same time, it is incumbent upon each woman to choose the perpetuation of
Judaism as a prime responsibility.
I feel it is important that you understand the true meaning of Sisterhood: we are able
to create and foster Jewish sentiment in the home, further Jewish education in the
family, sponsor and encourage the youth programs that insure our heritage, and
participate in activities to strengthen our relationship with our community and Israel.
It is our desire to keep you informed through reports of Social Action, Israeli Affairs
and Jewish Family Living. You may want to participate in a special interest group such
as Creative Handicrafts or join our Sisterhood Bat Mitzvah class. In addition our
Educational Activities Committee lets you select the area of study that stimulates your
intellectual and cultural growth. At the same time that you are enjoying activities of
your choice and learning new skills, you will be meeting new people and forming
lifelong friendships.
Center Sisterhood welcomes you to participate in another productive year.
I want to extend to you and your families my wishes for a Happy and Healthy New
Year.
Fondly,
Rene'e Gordon

Sisterhood Salutes the Gift Shop Committee
We recognize the Gift Shop committee
for the many hours and years of dedica¬
tion they have given on behalf of Center
Sisterhood. These women have earned
for themselves and for our Sisterhood a
record of great distinction. The gift
shop has always been well-stocked with
a varied selection of Judaica, of fine
craftmanship, most of which have been
made in Israel. Ceremonial objects,
holiday gifts and decorations, educa¬
tional materials and religious articles
are readily available.
We thank the following ladies for
their tireless efforts on behalf of the
Sisterhood Gift Shop. Their talents and
knowledge have made the shop one of
the finest in Jacksonville.

COUPLE’S
CLUBS
The Couple’s Clubs of the Center invite all Jewish couples of the community to join them and
to become part of a fun-loving, spirited and congenial group.
Both groups are strictly social and rarely raise funds. Their goals are to provide pleasant
environments where Jewish couples can get together and have enjoyable evenings at a
reasonable cost.
Couples’ Club
The Center Couples’ Club is newly formed and continues to attract new couples who are 35
and over. If you fall into this age group call Karen or Lou Nathanson, 737-5411.
There is no reason why you should be lonely on a Saturday night, come join us.
Ha’Rayim
What do wine and cheese parties, picnics, softball, Hawaiian Luau and friendship have to do
with Ha’Rayim? Everthing!
This is just a sample of what Ha’Rayim did last year. We are a groups of Jewish couples who
enjoy each other’s company. When we all get together, we always have fun regardless of what we
Center Couples’ Club
Karen & Lou Nathanson
do.
This year is going to be our most exciting ever. Several of our new members have brought fresh
ideas and enthusiasm to our group.
We are not a fund raising organization. Our goal is to provide a pleasant environment where
Jewish couples can get together and have an enjoyable time for a reasonable amount. Please join
us for our next function. There will be one soon!
Newly married and young couples within the 20-35 year old age span are welcome to join
HA’RAYIM. Call Bonnie or Neil Sandler for information, 731-1143.
Mr. & Mrs. Club: For mature & lively couples
- see page 227
Ha’Rayim
Bonnie & Neil Sandler

Jacksonville welcomes return of Leb’s
Jacksonville’s old-timers remember
when the name Leb’s Restaurant was
synonymous with good eating, and it was
a disappointment to many when it closed
in the late 1960s.
Now those who enjoyed its fine cuisine
can take heart. The restaurant has been
reborn on the Southside as LiP Leb’s, on
Baymeadows Road just off San Jose
Boulevard.
Ira Sachs, the owner of LiP Leb’s,
knows what kind of act he has to follow.
“I was affiliated with them when I was
just getting started in the restaurant
business,” he says. “I was their steward
when they opened in the old Roosevelt
Hotel in 1946.
“I was with them until 1948, when I left
and moved to Atlanta. There, I was in
charge of food at the Progressive Club.”
Sachs remembers that Leb’s was
considered one of the best restaurants in
Jacksonville. “In my own opinion, it was
the finest in town—no question about it,”
he said. “It was just a fantastic place.”
LiP Leb’s, which opened in mid
December, is quite smaller than its
predecessor and its menu is more
limited.
“They served many more meals than
we do,” says Sachs. “We mainly serve
sandwiches, soups, delicatessen items,
and we have a bakery.”
There is a wide assortment of
sandwiches and other items to choose
from. The menu lists, among others:
imported ham; corned beef; pastrami,
chopped liver; roast beef; turkey; tuna
salad, and chicken salad. A combo, with
your choice of three fillings, is also
offered.
Diners can help themselves to the free
kosher-style salad bar, offering fresh-
barrel sauerkraut, fresh-barrel half-sour
pickles, plum tomatoes and pickled
beets.
All sandwiches are served on the
patron s choice from among six breads,
all baked on the premises: regular rye;
onion rye; eggroll; onion roll; kaiser roll;
and pumpernickel.
Sachs is not the only alumnus of Leb’s
working at the new place.
The head chef at Leb's, Russel
Reinhart, met his wife, Iris, while she was
a waitress there. He’s my head chef here,
now, and Iris is head waitress,” Sachs
said. “And Joann Starr, another waitress
at Leb’s, is working for me now.”
The Reinharts also worked with Sachs
in Atlanta after he moved there.
Sachs returned to Florida in 1950 to
work in Miami Beach, first at
Pumpernick’s and then the Virginian.
Reinhart also returned to Florida. “He
opened every Wolfie’s,” Sachs said.
“That’s a restaurant franchise with
outlets through the state, mostly in
South Florida.”
Sachs said he decided to open LiP
Leb’s “for my two sons, Mike and Jeff.”
Michael Sachs is manager of the
restaurant, while Jeff is working as the
sandwich and salads cook.
3877 Baymeadows Road

The World of Our Grandparents
Children of the shtetl Cheder shuckle over their
Aleph Bet
“The World of Our Grandparents/’ like the world it depicted so beautifully, is
now history. But like the shtetl “niggun” — the shtetl melody, the music and the
memory linger on. The “happening” which transported literally thousands of
visitors to the shtetl of Eastern Europe was one of the most extraordinary
events to take place at the Center and in the community, for as long as one can
remember. The event was a success beyond all expectations.
The special beauty of the “World of Our Grandparents” was in the joy it brought
to so many people of different ages . . . the participation of adults and children,
staff and lay people, the “bubbas” and the “zeydes,” the “tatehs” and “mamas”
and the “kinderlach”. Everyone experienced the living of life of days gone by, in
food and in costume, in language and in texture . . . the life of Eastern Europe.
Rabbi Kentoi thanks Barney Margolin, who co-ordinated the
talent and Suzi Roey who co-ordinated WOGP
The Sabbath booth radiated a special warmth
The fourth graders presented a Passover skit which included many songs
Our gratitude is extended to the countless workers
and volunteers who served throughout the day.
Without the contributions of time and energy oi each
individual, we could never have made the shtetl the
success that it was.
But these are only the stars and the stage hands. The
real credits for all the planning go to Rabbi Dov
Kentof for his vision and ingenuity ... for his unique
ability to dream and implement, for his willingness to
work tirelessly the many weeks that it took to make
this dream a reality. Rabbi Kentof was “our producer”
and at his side kudos are extended to his director,
Suzi Roey, who coordinated, planned, worked and
labored to achieve this extraordinary success.
Another outstanding facet of the “shtett” experience
was the delicious variety of home-made foods served.
We are indebted to Lois Markowtiz for co¬
ordinating this extraordinary project.

Center Choral Society
Directed by
Cantor Emeritus Abraham Marion
Our Cantor Emeritus, Abraham Marton, held his Choral Society Concert, and delighted his entire audience. The selection
of music, the professional manner in which it was presented, and the entire program was a delight for all who were there. We
were enthralled with the wide range of voices and everyone rose and ebbed with each strain. It is indeed our good fortune to have
Cantor Marton, who is still our leading force in Jewish music at the Jacksonville Jewish Center, and we wish him many more
years and many more concerts. Outstanding Center talents, Raymond Cohen, tenor and soprano Rosalind Klein were also
íeatured at the concert.

rimyanaire
WHO ARE THE
MINYANAIRES?
WHAT IS THE
MINYANAIRE
PROGRAM?
WHY DO
MINYANAIRES
ATTEND?
WHO DO WE
NEED?
Minyanaires are men and women of all ages and life styles who attend one, some or every
Minyanaire Program.
The Minyanaire Program is held most Sundays at the Jacksonville Jewish Center. It begins
at 9 a.m. with a service held in the intimate atmosphere of the Witten Chapel. This is followed
by breakfast and the speaker of the day. We feature speakers on a variety of subjects that will
inform, excite, or entertain you. All Minyanaire Programs are free of chatge.
Minyanaires attend for various reasons. The brief service is inspirational. The friendship and
comraderie felt at breakfast is a pleasant change of pace from our usual morning routine. It’s
an opportunity to see old friends and meet new ones. There is no membership or on-going
obligation. Some may choose only a few programs to attend—some are there every week.
YOU!
We cordially invite you to try the Center’s Minyanaire Program. For more information call
Barry Minkoff — 73T5072.
A Babysitter is available during all Minyanaire Programs.
***SPECIAL NOTE to new or inactive Center members ... If you would like to become
invloved in any aspect of THE MINYANAIRE PROGRAM, we would love to have you on our
committee. Just call Barry and say “I’ll help”.

Jacksonville Chevra Kaddisha
Officers Standing: Alan Taffet, Nathan Sandier,
Simon Selber; Seated: Joel Bressler, Bea Browdy
Standing: Bea Browdy, Max Shmunes, Nathan Shmunes, Nathan Sandler, Simon
Selber, Joel Bressler, Dr. Michael Greenburg, Babs Goldstein. Seated: Dr. Cheryl
Rosenblatt, Ann Shmunes, Laurie Benwick, Esther Fain (Talpalar), Linda Bressler
and Lil Meizlik
The officers are: President - Joel Bressler; Vice-President - Nathan Sandler; Treasurer - Alan Taffet; Secretary Chary
Greenberg; Telephone chairperson - Ann and Nathan Shmunes. Other members are:Linda Bressler, Bea Browdy, Laurie
Benwick, Esther Fain (Talpalar), Babs Goldstein, Michael Greenburg, David Mizrahi, Max Shmunes, Simon Selber, Anita
Solomon, and Cheryl Rosenblatt.
Music, Flowers, Fancy Clothes, and a guest of honor-a-party? No, An American Funeral complete with a fancy brass coffin
hermetically sealed on embalmed, and made-up contents meant to withstand the wear and tear of time. Volumes have been
written about the mentally unhealthy aspects of the American funeral’s trappings that are all denials of death in one form or other.
By contrast the Jewish way of mourning is not only psychologically sound but is actually helpful and healing by allowing death to
be confronted realistically and grief to be worked through its stages. The Jewish way of death is one part of a philosophy of life in
which all people are endowed with dignity and given respect. Even after death, the body which once held a human soul retains its
sanctity. Our sages have compared a corpse to an impared Torah scroll which, although no longer useable, retains its holiness.
The body of the deceased is attended by a shomer (guard) from death until burial. The importance of this mitzva is emphasized
by the fact that the shomer is relieved of all other religious obligations during his watch. The tahara (ritual washing) and dressing
in the tachrichim (shrouds) which are plain white linen garments, are done by a committee of the Chevra Kaddisha. Then the
body is placed in a plain wooden coffin. The tachrichim and the plain wooden coffin have become a symbol of the equality we all
share in death. Their use was instituted more that 2000 years ago by the Rambam Gamliel who expressed his desire to be buried
in the plain linen shrouds and in an unadorned casket.
Since burial is prompt, usually within 24 hours, no embalming, or cosmetic work or public viewing is allowed. These practices,
along with cremation, are viewed by the Jewish community around the world as mutilation and desecration of the human
remains. The Jewish community also discourages the use of flowers at the funeral or in the home where shiva is being observed.
It is customary that the money ordinarily spent on fancy flowers, clothes and caskets be spent on the favorite charity of the
person who has died.
The work of the Chevra Kaddisha is considered a chesed shel emet, an act of loving kindness performed without ulterior
motive because the dead cannot repay this act of kindness. The Jacksonville community is fortunate to have a functioning
Chevra Kaddisha active since 1974. The members perform the necessary tasks on a rotating basis in committees of two men or
two women. New members are welcome and needed to continue these services important to our fellow Jews.
Meyer & Beth Leibo
in memory of his mother
Ellen Leibo
Lane & Erica Leibo
in memory of their grandmother
Ellen Leibo

Henry Kramer Library
And
Resource Center
COMMITTEE MEMBERS
Chairman Minnie Schreiber
Co-Chairman TobiMeisel
Committee May Goldie Stillman, Diane Fages,
Lillian Meizlik, Eva Weinshelbaum,
Hermine Newman, Florence Schreiber,
Gene Kornblum, Lauren Rothstein,
Roselyn Selber
Minnie Schreiber
Chairman-Library Committee
Our attractive library has been beautifully furnished and library equipped throughthe
generosity of Mrs. Henry Kramer. It has been the work of our staff and our volunteers
that has made it possible for our tremendous progress from our modest beginning in 1955 on 3rd and Silver Streets. Roselyn
Selber started our first Library - aSunday School Library. One of her coworkers was Charlotte Glossman, blessed memory, who
served as our Librarian for twenty years. Her dedication and devotion was a major factor in the growth of our Library.
In memory of Charlotte Glossman a special book circulation program after Shabbat services was provided during the
summer under the direction of Mrs. Lillian Meizlik.
Meanwhile volunteer committee members May Goldie Stillman and Diane Fages have devoted many hours to insure the
smooth operation of the adult collection.
The Solomon Schechter Day School was kept open several months in the beginning of the school year by a group of
mothers and grandmothers under the direction of Lauren Rothstein. Volunteers Sharon Witten, Eva Weinshelbaum, Carol
Schneider, Shirley Bielski, Maxine Funk, Debbie Gottlieb, Ginger Proctor, Marilyn Mass and Lauren Rothstein kept the Library
open for the children to enjoy reading. We obtained the services of Mrs. Nora Freedman who served beautifully as a part time
Librarian for the rest of the school year.
The Solomon Schechter Day School added many new books to the shelves of the Day School Library with a gift book
certificate program honoring special events. The PTA was most generous in their contributions of time and money. As a result
the teachers and the children enjoyed the many new books, and the time they spent in the library was an important factor in the
learning process.
Jewish Book Month was celebrated with the dedication of the Rita Gendzier Judaica collection purchased with funds
contributed to the Rita Gendzier Library Fund. This was spearheaded by a Library subcommittee, Florence Schreiber, Hermine
Newman and Tobi Meisel, Chairman.

CEMETERY COMMITTEE
OF THE
Jacksonville Jewish Center
KENNETH BOWDEN
General Manager
JACK BECKER
Co-Chairman
JOE BECKER
Co-Chairman
The Cemetery Committee provides a very necessary function of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. It is very important that
the Cemetery grounds are well kept and provide a peaceful resting place for our loved ones.
Our Cemetery Committee is co-chaired by Joe Becker and Jack Becker. Serving on their committee are Max Becker,
Hymie Selber, Alan Taffet, Dr. Joe Witten, Dr. Sam Witten, Jack Bettman and Joel Bressler.
Mr. Kenneth A. Bowden, Controller of the Jacksonville Jewish Center, also serves as general manager of our Cemetery. It
is his responsibility to oversee the upkeep and maintenance of the grounds. He has an excellent working relationship with
Mr. Jennewine who is general manager of Evergreen Cemetery. Mr. Jennewine has been very generous with his time and his
staff, Mr. Campbell, Mr. Miller and Mrs. Phillips. They have all taken excellent care of the Center’s property, above and beyond
what is expected. We appreciate everything they do.
Johnny B. Mercer has been custodian of our Cemetery for over 20 years. Mr. Bergerson of Southern Monument Co., has
always been extremely helpful to us, too. We are very grateful to all these people who have given so generously of their time.

===== PAGE 90 =====
Solomon Schechter Day School
An increasing number of students in our community are now experiencing
two quality educations: a dynamic General Studies program which far
exceeds the public school offerings integrated with an intensive and multi¬
dimensional Judaic Studies program. The effect on the children is readily
apparent. Our children are proud to be Jews and have their Jewish self¬
esteem supported by the backbone of religious knowledge and practice.
They identify with and support Israel and their mastery of the Hebrew
language continues to astound us.
The children have developed a love of learning that transcends any one
subject area. Most importantly, they are learning values that are incumbent
on us as parents, educators, and members of the community to pass on to
future generations: self-respect, respect for one another, respect for those
who are different, the importance of community. We feel that the Solomon
Schechter Day School is an enormous asset to this community.
It is our belief that the greatest impact of the Solomon Schechter Day School
lies in the future. Jewish leaders, professional, religious, and lay, assert that
day schools hold one of the very best answers to furthering Jewish
continuity.
We are very proud to be able to say that our school has a student body
ranging from the Jumping Jacks (our 2 year old program), Pre-School (3 and
4 year olds), Kindergarten, (5 year olds), to our Day School, first through the
seventh year grades. We look forward to each year with enthusiasm and
excitement as our numbers grow and add to our accomplishments.
Marilyn Sandler
Principal
Suzi Roey
Assistant to Administrator
of the Supplemental Schools
Evelyn Borstein
School Secretary
HEBREW DEPARTMENT
Alina Kentof
liana Manasse

===== PAGE 100 =====
Sunday School
The Sunday School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is
planned expressly for children in Kindergarten, First and
Second Grades. A two hour program provides each child the
opportunity to experience the richness of their Jewish
Heritage through song, dance, role playing, cooking, short
stories, games, arts and crafts. Emphasis is placed on the
holidays and festivals. Jewish values and mitzvot are
encouraged.
This year the Sunday School was under the direction of
Rabbi Dov Kentof and is also coordinated by Tricia Bloom.

The Afternoon Hebrew School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is designed for students in grades 3 - 7 and provides an
elementary education leading to Bar/Bat Mitzvah and continuation in the high school program.
Meeting twice during the week and on Saturdays, “the basics” are at the core of the curriculum, with stress on Hebrew
Reading, Jewish Social Studies, and current events relating to Israel are discussed thoroughly. Synagogue skills, holiday
observances and knowledge of prayers are all part of the comprehensive Afternoon Hebrew Schoool program.

Torah Readers
Not all progress is achieved by sudden leaps and bounds. Steady growth has many
unseen advantages. Regular worshipppers at Shabbat and Holiday services can observe
subtle advancements in quality of Torah Reading during the past year. This is a sign of
incalculable value for the beautification of the Mitzvah of public worship.
Bar Mitzvah was an incentive for individualized study has long been recognized, but is
quickly dissipated. The educational challenge lies, therefore, in arranging a pattern for
extended development. Of all synagogue skills only Torah reading allows for non-
repetitive, exacting preparation for the close to 500 different portions read through the year. The Torah schroll contains
no punctuation marks, vowels, accents, or portion endings. These must be laboriously memorized. A large percentage
of our Bar Mitzvah candidates are willing to meet this challenge and to continue to participate in this specialty.
The total list of readers, young and old, who recognize that reading the Torah is a self rewarding Mitzvah of high value,
has grown this year by the addition of the following Bar Mitzvahs: Haim Becker, Bart Chepenik, Bruce Gelman,
Jonathan Gray, Howard Markowitz, Michael Rosenblatt and Bradley Rosenthal.
We are thankful to all the readers who have continued to devote much of their time to this activity. They have made
attendance at services more meaningful.
Ted Gross

Jacksonville
Jewish Center
Day Camp 1981
Where everyday is an adventure
OUR GOALS
Camping can be one of the most meaningful experiences
a child will ever have. Our Camp offers not only a
situation in which children can learn and develop by
interacting with other children, but it offers a full scope of
activites designed to make a child aware of our Jewish
heritage, to develop new friendships, enjoy the wonders
of nature that may be found on our large wooded
campsite and the importance of active sports.
The Center Day Camp is unique in the fact that few other
camps offer such a wide variety of activities at such a
nominal cost. Now is the time to enroll your children. We
do hope that they will join us for the eight exciting weeks
of summer fun and growth.

COUNSELORS -IN-TRAINING
(C.I.T’s)
Our C.l.T. program is for a group of interested and
mature 8th and 9th graders. Counselor-in-training is part
of a balanced program that includes training through
speakers, discussion groups, supervised experience
working with campers, and enjoyable recreational
activities. The program emphasizes self-development, as
well as the developments of camp skills, the dynamics of
working with groups and individual guidance.

Philosophy
Through its programs the Jewish Center seeks to foster individual
growth and development, encourage participation in community life,
provide opportunity for friendship, fun, and the acquisition and
expression of Jewish knowledge and identification.
To achieve these objectives, the Jewish Center employs the services
of professionally-trained staff as well as experts in a variety of related
fields.
The Center’s 36 acre complex contains full and comprehensive
facilities for cultural arts, community forums, social, athletic and club
activites.
Other classes included Tumbling, Creative Crafts, Cheerleading and
Baton, Teen Beat, Make-up and Volleyball. Adults also had an
opportunity to participate in “Stress Management classes” and
“Bridging the Generation Gap”, a parenting course which also included
teenagers.

Neal F. Portnoy Youth Lounge
The Neal F. Portnoy Youth Lounge was made possible
through the generosity of the Louis Portnoy family. This well-
equipped and well designed facility is used to serve the needs of
the youth of our congregation and the Jewish youth of the
entire community. It is the central location for formal
meetings, programs and activities. It also serves as a
synagogue for youth services.
Equipped with all sorts of games, such as pinball
machines, foozball, bumper pool, electronic and board games,
the youth lounge has something for everybody.
During March, which is National Physical Education and Sports Month, the schools of the Center promoted physical
fitness with Jump Rope for Heart. The event was sponsored by the Florida Association for Health, physical education
and recreation and our schools to benefit the American Heart Association. Sponsors paid each participant according to
the time each jumped rope. Many parents and students participated.
We are very proud of the $950.00 raised by our students: Alyse Slaff, David Lazarus, Eileen Greenbaum, Marc and
Andrew Selevan, Melissa Broadman, Ellis Mass, David Silverman, Lee Pollock, Jacob Sail, Craig Kornblum, Douglas
Witten and Wendy Jackler.
Special thanks to the parents who assisted: Cindy Aronsohn, Ellen Schnipper, Susan Alterman, Maxine and Jerry
Funk, Sharon Witten, Eva Jackler, Judy Davis, Idlene Slaff and Rose Lynn Plotkin.
Jumpers who collected the most were: Marc and Andrew Selevan, Douglas Witten and Wendy Jackler.

Main Street, has enjoyed its most successful year. Several business establishments have been extremely helpful to us by
donating good merchandise. Members also have contributed clothing and a variety of good saleable items.
Special thanks to the following people who have helped us make the store such a good fund raising program for our
Center - - Leonard Setzer, Ronald Cherry, Ralph Simon, Ted Merlin, Sid Garfinkel, Jay Carter, F.B. Scent, Jr., Morris
Leibo, Harry Lasris, Robert Lasris, Mr. Horesal, Mr. Dent McGhee, Mr. Penquinot, Mrs. Rose Hochman.
As we look to the new year we ask everyone’s suppoi £. Call the Center Office (268-6736) for pickup.

customer.

Doo Kentof
DÍRectoR op Eócicatíon & Yocitto
Rabbi Dov Kentof
With the coming of the new school year, I am most excited and pleased
with the promise of an even more intensive and creative program for all
of our students. Included in the curriculum will be the increased
awareness of the Jewish current events as well as improved reading
skills and knowledge of the prayer book. Our students should become
more actively involved in the practice of daily ritual and holiday observance as a result of the creative approach our
teachers will utilize in their classrooms.
As our numbers increase in the Junior Congregation and Shabbat Classes, it is most assuring to note the pride
which each of our students is gaining as they strive for perfect attendance and to have their name on the Rabbi’s
list. But it is most rewarding to see so many young people actively involved in a Saturday morning service -- each of
them having the opportunity to participate and to recognize and acknowledge the feeling of love of Torah.
This year the Judaica High School students will have a “new” adventure into education with the advent of dynamic
speakers and assemblies. Also, our staff is a very strong one and eager to take on the challenge of our young
people. The curriculum should excite and motivate those students who will take advantage of the opportunity that
awaits each of them.
Sunday School is a most innovative and creative approach to our young people Kindergarten through Second
grade. Included is an introduction to Hebrew, an exploration of our history through various Bible Heroes, and a
study of the ritual and holiday observance.
Our most positive statement for our youth is the leadership of our youth groups. Together, the advisors, boards,
and I have planned a calendar of fun, challenge and spiritually meaningful and dynamic programming for
CHALUTZIM, KADIMA and USY. All of the groups will be working together on a synagogue mitzvah day, and
with canoe trips, Simchat Torah, Honey Cake packages for Mt. Carmel, family retreats, Raising your Parents,
Who am I?, Inter-faith Seder, Teenage Values, our youngsters will be busily engaged in a balanced program of
educational, spiritual, and fun-loving activities in the atmosphere of our synagogue and Jewish home.
It is my hope that our programs will touch some of you or maybe “most” of you, and that you and your family will
help us grow as we try to broaden your children’s Jewish horizons.
Alina, Tali, Gil and I would like to take this opportunity to wish you a healthy and happy New Year.
Best regards,
Rabbi Dov Kentof
Director of Education/Youth

“Kadimaniks show their spirit at the recent Convention”
One of the many field trips taken by Kadima was “tubing” down the
Itchunutuknee.
Jonathan Gray was the first to take off
Objectives
1. To recognize the importance of Shabbat and holiday
observance.
2. To recognize the need for regular devotion both individually
and in groups.
3. To develop an appreciation and willingness to work for the
synagogue.
4. To increase the use of Hebrew in informal settings.
5. To establish a communal sense by supporting community
and civic activities.
6. To recognize the importance of Israel as a modern center for
world Jewry.
7. To develop a Jewish identity through music and dance.
8. To develop an appreciation of Jewish life.
KADIMA
General Aim: Jewish families today look to the synagogue
as the center for Jewish activity: religious, educational,
social and recreational. A major goal of the synagogue and
its school is the education of youth with a firm base of
knowledge; Jewish identification of youth, a sense of pride
in their heritage, and a committment to Judaism. The goal
of Kadima is to bring about the internalization of Jewish
values, attitudes and behavior patterns by supplementing
the child’s formal religious education with informal Jewish
learning experience.

United Synagogue Youth
U.S.Y.
U.S.Y. General Aim
The United Synagogue Youth program’s aim is to bring about a
meaningful and fully reciprocal encounter of Judaism, the Jewish people,
and the synagogue on one side, and the Jewish teenager on the other. As a
result of this encounter, the Jewish teenager will, firstly demonstrate and
experience how the Jewish way of living through study, action and
fellowship leads the way to personal fulfillment and growth while meeting
his spiritual and social needs, and secondly, make a significant
contribution to his synagogue and the Jewish people, his community and
society.
Jodi Roey
President
Incoming Officers and Board of Kadima & U.S.Y.

Summer is for
. . MEANINGFUL JEWISH EXPERIENCES AT CAMP RAMAH
—the most fabulous experience of their teenage lives.
A place to meet Jewish kids and adults from many
parts of the country and Israel in an atmosphere of
Jewish Learning and Jewish Life as they have never
lived before . . . with SHABBAT they will never forget.
Ramah is a quality summer life for your children ... it is
friendships, learning and fun. It is “Guys and Dolls” in
Hebrew with singing and dancing costumes and laughter. RAMAH is a dining
room thanking God for our food after a meal, and minutes later songs and
excitement for evening activity announcements. RAMAH helps our kids feel
comfortable as Jews - and this lasts a lifetime - think about RAMAH for your
children entering grades 5 through grade 11 - for the summer of 1982. Think
about RAMAH as a gift that could help a child to make choices and decisions in
a difficult world.

. . . TOURING THE U S A. WITH U.S.Y.
Janet Jacobson
When we think of summer our thoughts turn to summer camp for our high school
students, who have grown out of the usual sleep-away camps. Why not investigate
the possibilities of “USY on Wheels?” This program is designed for high school
students and provides the opportunity for students to travel as a group across the
country, well-chaperoned with teenagers from all parts of the U.S.
The trip is geared to the interests of teenagers. The group goes coast-to-coast, seeing Big Sur and San Francisco,
spending Shabbat outdoors in a national park, and visiting many parts of our country. Most of all, they do it as
Jews, learning that one can see America and be a Jew, that they can have fun as observant Conservative Jews.
. . . EXPERIENCING ISRAEL
. . . Not for a summer, but for a lifetime.
The USY Israel Pilgrimage 1981 is a summer in Israel which incorporates living contemporary Judaism, including
Shabbat and Kashrut, with the study of their heritage.
In addition to spending a delightful summer with their Israeli peers, our Pilgrims also develop lifelong friendships
with their traveling companions from some of the finest homes in the United States and Canada - close associa¬
tions which continue upon the Pilgrims return to their homes and form everlasting bonds with their Israeli friends.
USY pilgrims stay in hotels and youth villages . . . and are given opportunities to relate to everyday life in Israel...
to observe the blending of diverse backgrounds into a united people ... to understand the facts and problems of
life in Israel... to visit the Old City of Jerusalem, the Art Colony in Tzfat... a boat ride on the Kinneret. These are
just a sampling of what your children can experience.

CUB SCOUTS PACK 14
Cub scouting is an introduction to Boy Scouts and plays an outstanding role for boys between the ages of 8 and 10. The boys
advance from Wolf to Bear and then on to Webelos. In this day and age when there are so many temptations, our boys find
themselves interested in completing their achievements toward a higher rank, and participating in the monthly pack meeting.
There, they put on skits and show off their craft. During the year, they share in many new and different experiences -- Annual
Pinewood and Rocket Derby, Scout Sabbath, and the Blue and Gold Banquet. Each year, the LARRY HACKER CUB SCOUT
AWARD is presented to the Cub with the best attendance. His name is placed on a permanent plaque that remains at the
Center.
The Center is very indebted to Mr. Lanny Zimmerman for all of his dedication to the Cub Scout program, and know that he will
be needing your support for the coming year. If you can be of some help, please call Mrs. Shelly Kulchin at the Jewish Center.

Boy Scout Troop #14 is for boys ages 11-17. The troop has
been in existence 60 years and is among the oldest in the state
of Florida. Scouting activities include numerous outdoor
camping activities- learning of many skills and participation in
programs which contribute to the spiritual, physical, and
emotional growth of the individual boys.
Experienced scoutmaster, Randall Silverberg and assistant
scoutmaster Billy Wilensky provide excellent leadership for
the troop.

Brownie Troop #147
This year Brownie Troop #147 has
enjoyed a vast number of activities.
In addition to a trip to the museum
and planetarium, we have had such
projects as making a vase, planting
seeds, going on a nature hike and
many more. Our troop consists of all
faiths and includes first through
third graders. We meet weekly and
our meetings are both fun and
educational.

Girl Scout Troop #162
Girl Scouting is more than a club, it is a nationwide, worldwide
movement for girls ages 7-17. Girl Scouts make a promise and
live by the Girl Scout laws. These simple guides prepare a young
girl to live in our bewildering modern world.
GIRL SCOUT PROMISE
On my honor, I will try;
To do my duty to God and my country,
To help other people at all times,
To obey the Girl Scout laws.
Troop 162 has fulfilled all of the obligations of scouting throughout
the year under the leadership of Clara Mann. Arts and crafts
camping, working on all badges has made some very worthwhile
experiences. Together the girls worked on many badges such as
troop camping, cooking, active citizen, which included a tour of
the Police building.
The troop had a great time bowling once a month and is making
up their own badge for it. Our troop also takes pride in being the
third top salesgirls for the annual cookie sale.
If you are interested in being a Scout or a leader, call Clara Mann
at 733-6472.

The merriment and all participation of the holiday of Purim can be felt in the Synagogue while the Megillah is being read. Always a
capacity crowd, the holiday creates a joyous atmosphere the children remember from year to year. The sound of the grogger and
dangling jewlery, beards and mustaches, three-cornered hats all add to the excitement. The following pictures were taken this
past year.

The Jacksonville Jewish Federation made a commitment several years ago to
enhance the quality of Jewish life throughout our community. This past year saw
the onset of several major programs which have moved the community
significantly closer to the type of Jewish community we would all like to live in.
Many of these programs are under the auspices of the Dept, of Community
Services. This Dept, has had a major staff change with Iris Tekel assuming the
position of Director of Community Services.
During this past year, the Dept, of Community Services was responsible for
the implementation of a Kosher Nutrition Program. The program has become a
model throughout Duval County. The program was established through a
coalition developed by the Federation and included Jewish Family Children’s
Services and its Director, Larry, Reckow, as well as River Garden Hebrew Home
for the Aged and its Director, Elliott Palevsky, and Staff Associate, Martin Goetz.
The Community Services Dept, also coordinated several significant programs
for community youth which were again addressed through a community coalition
process. Rabbi Dov Kentof chaired a Community Youth Program involving all four
congregations as well as unaffiliated youth. This program was funded through and
coordinated by the Federation. In addition, Rabbi Marc Volk chaired a similar
coalition involving the Educators of the four congregations and of Gan Yeladim.
Federation funds were translated into a dynamic community teacher training
program.
During the coming year the Federation will be moving into its new office
building on Old St. Augustine Rd. Our Community Services will expand
considerably, particularly in the areas of family life education. These programs will
be provided in a variety of formats working with and through the four
congregations, River Garden and Jewish Family & Children’s Services. We will
also expand our Leadership Development efforts so that future leadership will be
cultivated and available when needed.
The Federation will continue to conduct the Annual Combined Jewish Appeal
which raises monies for our local agencies as well as the United Jewish Appeal and
national organizations. The Federation has made major increases in its
commitment to both our local Jewish Family & Children’s Services and the River
Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged. Our Russian Resettlement program
continues to be a model program throughout the southeast part of the United
States. Federation programs will continued to provide services in areas such as
Senior Citizen programming, Single Parent Family programming, College Age
programs, Jewish Singles in their 30’s and 40’s, etc.
The Federation provides a vehicle for service to our community and it is the
hope of the Officers and Board that we unify our Jewish community for the
betterment of all individuals and organizations.

Lawrence Rackow
Executive Director
Founded in 1917, as the United Jewish Charities, J.F.C.S. is the oldest of the three social service agencies serving the Jewish
community. Its goals, as described in its by-laws, are to strengthen and/or restore family functioning and stability, to enable
individuals to approach their maximum potential and to help individuals and families lead happier and healthier lives.
In 1980, 880 individuals were served in one or more of its four programs, an increase of 355 persons over 1979. With the
establishment of the Kosher Nutrition Program the senior citizen programs increased by 284 to 490 individuals served.
Counselling and the New American programs increased by 47 to 254. With the further anticipated cut-backs in federal aid
programs, the demands on the agency in the coming year are expected to continue increasing. At this point we have no firm
knowledge of how many new New Americans can be expected.
Nathaniel Greenwood, a well trained, experienced, certified social worker joined the staff in June. His work is focussing on family
and individual counselling and Family Life Education. The priorities for the latter, recommended by the Planning Committee, are
single parent families, teenagers, and senior citizens. This committee further recommended that a formal job file be established
to aid our clients to locate work as quickly as possible and to aid employers in finding well qualified help. Your cooperation in this
worthwhile project is earnestly solicited.
L’Shona Tova Tecotevu
Lawrence Rackow

Lewis Meisel
“HEMSHEKH” and River Garden
“Hemshekh” as an ongoing reality is central to Jewish life. The word, simply translated, means “continuity.” Throughout
our history, it has been imbued with all of the meanings and qualities necessary for Jewish survival. It brings to mind an
unbroken thread, woven into many patterns, creating over time a fabric of integrity and evolving beauty. River Garden is
both a place of “Hemshekh” and a source of “Hemshekh.”
To allow for the continuation of Jewish life at every level, the Home observes Kashruth, the Sabbath and holidays. The rich
activity programming is shaped by aspects of Jewish culture, celebration and community involvement. If this program of
religious activity is to be maintained, members of the several local congregations must fulfill their obligation to attend and
participate regularly in the religious services held at River Garden. The River Garden Chapel was recently dedicated in honor
of Dr. and Mrs. Sam Witten on the occasion of their 50th anniversary.
Many residents have a deep need to attend religious services yet are no longer able to perform these services, nor are there
sufficient able men to form a Minyan. Special care needs can be met within the community because of River Garden. In turn, it
is up to the community to make it possible for River Garden to remain a place of Jewish “Hemshekh.”
River Garden is also a place of continuity, on a person-to-person level. Some ’’homes may have as a goal, relieving the older
resident of all personal responsibility and relieving the families of ongoing inter-personal responsibility. At River Garden, our
goal is to provide for and do that which the resident (and family) are incapable of doing due to chronic disabilities or short term
problems, in order to allow each resident to continue living as fully as possible, using existing areas of strength and ability. We
will not patronize of infantilize our elders by taking over those functions or responsibilities which they are capable of handling.
To do the latter, even when requested, would result in a loss of self-respect and sense of competence. This is often followed by
the feeling that there is no reason to continue living. We will also not intrude upon the normal continuing responsibilities of
families toward their elder members. On the contrary, we will do all in our power to reinforce this important aspect of human
relations, which is central to the Jewish tradition. The mandate of the Fifth Commandment is a personal one.
The median age of the residents at River Garden is 86. We recognize that opportunities for continued growth and
development,the experience of newness, is important at every age. Therefore, we attempt to provide both opportunity and
motivation for such experience. We also recognize that the individual already has well-formed personality and character
traits. It is our goal to allow the person to continue comfortable patterns and meanings within the constraints of the
institutional community. This is not the time to reform or reshape, but to continue creatively. In all these way, River Garden is
a place of “Hemshekh.”
The Home is also a source of “Hemshekh.” Most families in small Jewish communities must look far from home to find a
facility that can meet special geriatric care needs within a Jewish environment. The fact that Jacksonville has a Home, the
quality, size and sophistication of River Garden means that close family ties and friendships can- continue within the local
setting. Most older people today receive needed medical supervision and help with activities of daily living in their own home.
The care givers are usually a more able spouse and/or the person’s children.
When care needs become so complex and intense that they cannot reasonably be met within the family and general
community, attempts at meeting such needs can become destructive to family and personal relationships. At this point, River
Garden becomes the care giver, making it possible for personal and familiar relationships to continue. The ongoing person-to-
person and inter-generational contact which River Garden thus makes possible, is vital to family continuity, the passing on of
Jewish values, traditions and identity.
The human service and resident care philosophies of the Home are deeply rooted in Jewish ethics, social and religious
values. Community support of the Home is an ongoing actualization of the Jewish values of human and social responsibility.
River Garden is itself a community in which Jewish living, activity and creativity take place. For many within the Jewish
community, working with River Garden as a trustee or volunteer is an important expression of their Jewish identity. The
larger community perceives River Garden as representative of Jewish social, ethical and service commitments.
River Garden reminds us and gives us the opportunity to continue vital elements of Jewish living both as individuals and as a
community. In short, “Hemshekh.”

Officers - 1981
River Garden Auxiliary
President Mrs. Stuart Walters
First Vice President Mrs. Jack Price
Second Vice President Mrs. Jack Dittman
One of the many pleasures of the Ladies Auxiliary of
River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged is to help the
residents retain that special feeling of a normal home and
social life.
Third Vice President Mrs. Rubin Janofsky
Financial Secretary Mrs. Mitchell Peltz
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Karl Frisch
Recording Secretary Mrs. Ted Freedman
Executive Secretary Mrs. Gerald Fisher
General Treasurer Mrs. Manual Katz
Parliamentarian Mrs. Sidney Entman
Through research in medicine and the competency of
our staff, the residents are able to enjoy a more complete
life-style. We hope that the evergrowing membership and
board will be able to continue this concept for many years.
Through the conscientious work of membership and
board of the Auxiliary, this year has been a very successful
one financially.
Among our sources of income are the following:
Memorial Plaques, Social Printing, Linen Shower, Tree of
Life, Tombola and Donor. The latest project is a
commerative plaque donated by the Bonnett Family in
memory of their parents. The motif “Honor Thy Mother
and Thy Father” which is the Fifth Commandment, is very
fitting. The tablet is on the West wall of River Garden for the
entire community as well as residents to admire.
As a result of our being able to raise our funds we have purchased terry robes which allow the residents to come from the
showers with dignity; new dining room chairs for greater comfort, and as of this writing, five whirlpools for therapy.
Our new social project being developed enables an Auxiliary member to take a resident “Out To Lunch”. The surprise
element brings added pleasure to the residents selected each month.
We are pleased to share special holiday celebrations with the residents: a carnival for Purim, a Channuka party and special
programs for Mothers Day and for Fathers Day.
Two years ago when our Volunteer Program started, there were only forty members and now it has grown to over 100.
There is an ever growing need for more volunteers to help the residents with their many needs. Please contact our Coordinator,
Eleanore Dorman at 389-3665 extension 232.
The fullest cooperation of the Auxiliary’s membership and board will insure a richer year for the residents of River Garden.
As incoming President, I look forward to working with our membership, board, staff and community toward a fuller and richer
year ahead. Marilyn Waters, President

Mount Carmel Gardens
Officers 81-82
President MitziShumer
1st Vice-Pres. .. Mary Foor
2nd Vice-Pres Della Billingsly
3rd Vice-Pres . George Blitzer
Secretary Mildred Shafner
Corresponding Secretary Edna Thomas
Treasurer Lona Isaac
5864 Mount Carmel Terrace
Jacksonville, Florida
Mount Carmel Gardens has become very well know in our Community-and the newspapers and television have occasionally
covered some of our numerous activities. Our volunteers are always ready with their services, be it to help in the Kosher
Nutrition Center; to act as teachers’ aids (we have the largest group of school volunteers in the city); help in Senior projects, such
as the MOB; roll bandages for the Cancer Society; work at Channel 7 T.V.; forming a chapter for A.A.R.P.; and collect monies
for charities. On our Brag Wall in our auditorium we have awards from Leukemia, Multiple Sclerosis, Muscular Dystrophy,
American Heart Association, Health and Rehabilitative Services and from our Mayor.
Among the many things done this year at Mount Carmel: the Mott Foundation Grant has put bus transportation within the reach
of all Seniors here; Fannie Kaufman received the National Award of $500, plus $500 for Englewood School, plus a big write-up in
50-Plus Magazine, for her work in the community; Elsie Casey was chosen outstanding painter and “Best in Show” at several art
exhibits; Israel Schemer was selected to play the star role in the publicity for the Mayor’s Older Buddies (MOB), playing on all
local television channels; and Arthur Stern was chosen “Citizen of the Year” at the Senior Citizen 20th Anniversary celebration
and was presented with a beautiful plaque.
The Carmel Entertainers have been recognized for their outstanding showmanship, under the direction of LaWanda Rampino,
with Fannie Kaufman accompanying. They have the honor of being the official group to entertain for the Mayor at City functions.
Our Carmel Singers also go out and entertain, under the direction of Jim Jarrard. Both the Singers and Entertainers are on call
and have brought much pleasure to many convalescent homes, organizations and groups, wherever or whenever they are
asked.
Our educational and social activities are many and here too, many volunteers contribute their work so that there is always fun
and interesting projects in which all may participate.
AH in all Mount Carmel is a large group of dedicated people, working together to make the best of everything possible for
everyone. We are indeed thankful for our many blessings.
Contributed by Pauline Schemer, for the 1981—1982 Center Year Book

Jack Becker presents Anne and Nathan Shmunes with
certificate of the Grove they planted in honor of their
grandchildren.
The Jacksonville District of the Zionist Organization of America has many activities including education, the promotion of
the State of Israel, public relations and is one of the most effective lobbying organizations nationally because of having a
constituency or membership.
The past year has been one of our busiest in many years. First, we are proud to announce that Alan Taffet is now President
of the Southeast Region of the Zionist Organization of America. In addition, we have sponsored two programs with Beth Shalom
Congregation. The first was an Aliyah Conference with the Regional Director of the Southeastern Aliyah Office from Miami. Our
second program was one of the most successful and effective we have had in years, a Conference on Jerusalem. This was
conducted by three well known scholars, a Catholic professor, Dr. Eugene J. Fisher, a Protest Minister, Dr. Marvin K. Wilson,
and Rabbi A. James Rudin of the American Jewish Committee. Not only was this very interesting and informative for the Jewish
community but they spoke to the Council of Churches the next day. This was very important to us as two of the most important
functions of the Z.O.A. are to foster American support for Israel and to counter the Arab propaganda campaign, and some of the
most influential people in town are the ministers.
Our oldest activity has been our Jewish National Fund function in which we yearly plant a grove of 1,000 trees in Israel. This
year was our best ever as we planted 2,400 trees. One grove was in memory of Gilbert Geller, 1,200 trees in honor of their
grandchildren by Nathan and Anne Shmunes, and 200 trees in memory of his family lost in the Holocaust by Cantor Hyman
Perras.

Hadassah
Hadassah, the largest Zionist organization in the world, as well as
the largest women’s voluntary organization in the United States, has a
membership of 360,000 members who dedicate themselves to help
Israel to help itself.
Our Jacksonville Chapter of Hadassah coordinates the activities
of our three groups: Tikva, which meets during the daytime, on the
second Tuesday, September through May; Hannah Senesch, which
meets on the second Wednesday evening; and Shir-Li, our youngest
group, which meets every third Monday evening.
We have just completed a successful year, with the help of each of
our members, having filled our committment National quotas. These
funds help support the Hadassah-Hebrew University Medical Center
and the Mt. Scopus Hospital in Jerusalem which service people of all
races and creeds. Here teaching and research are carried on which
benefit all mankind. Hadassah’s varied projects include the Hadassah
Medical Organization, Youth Aliyah, Hadassah Israel Educational
Services, Jewish National Fund, Zionist Youth Activities, and
American Affairs. Each of our groups develops its own program of
activities to support these projects, with the Chapter co-ordinating the
three groups.
We heartily welcome you, one and all, to join us, to get to know us,
and work with us. Please call our Presidents for further information.
Meanwhile, we wish you and your dear ones a healthy and happy year.
Shalom!

Shalom all!
You ask what AZA and BBYO have been doing this past year???
Conventions, car washes, progressive dinners, beach parties, meetings, sleep-
overs, Halloween party, creative services, Friday night Services at Mount Carmel
for Yom Hashoah, bowling tournament, basketball, frisbee, football, a pumpkin ^ , Tfljar
carve, a kissing booth at the World of Our Grandparents, phone soliciting for the
Federation Super Sunday, Pizza and Skating parties, lox boxes, Trips to Gainesville, mystery days, campouts and FUN.
We are the largest Jewish Youth Organization in the world. We have chapters in all the continental United States, in England,
France, Israel, Canada, Australia, South Africa and elsewhere in the world.
AZA is an international organization designed to develop both a Jewish identity and leadership qualities in teenage men. Every
member becomes a link in the tremendous chain of AZA’s brotherhood.
Before closing, I would like to thank our devoted advisors, Stanley Greenfield and Ed Silverman. Also, we encourage each and
EVERYONE of you to support AZA, as we support you when we faithfully serve our community.
If you are interested in joining AZA call me at 733-4315 or Ron Burman 268-0757.
SHALOM AND HAVE A HAPPY NEW YEAR!!!
Fraternally yours,
Jeff

B’nai B’rith Girls have been busy throughout the year.
We’ve had many programs including a “No Talk-A-
Thon”with the proceeds going to charity; an Oneg Shabbat
with Mt. Carmel; a surprise “Wake-up Breakfast” to initiate
our new members and lots more.
Front (L-R)- Barbara Sherman, Ellen Abramson, Peri Kantor, BethJawitz.
Middle - Pam Damesek, Jill Husney, Shelli Barrack (advisor), Barbara
Rosenberg. Standing - Sherri Lissner, Wendy Hirsch, Joy Pollock
We are looking forward to having speakers talk to us about the Holocaust, E.R.A. and stress management. Also, we are
anticipating to host a B’nai B’rith youth organization executive meeting in June. Our calendar has been very busy. What we are
hoping to do in the near future is to start programming with other Jewish youth groups in Jacksonville. This decision came out of
the concern that there seems to be a growing indifference among Jewish teenagers toward Judaism. We are now committed to
start programming on a larger and more “public” scale.
We want to prove that being Jewish can be a fun and learning experience at the same time. It certainly has been for us. For more
information on B.B.G. contact Peri Kantor at 733-1916.

Israel Kaplan Lodge #800 B’NAI B’RITH is an active organization in the Jewish
Community of Jacksonville. The lodge meets monthly with general meetings and
has dinners several times a year. Lodge membership is at the 275 mark for this
year. Various projects are held during the year to raise funds for B’NAI B’RITH
youth services. Hillel Foundations at the various state universities are supported
by B’NAI B’RITH as well as local A.Z.A. Chapters and B.B.G. Chapters. The local
lodge under the direction of Milton Hecht raised funds during the year 1979 and
purchased a torah for the Hillel Foundation at Florida State University in
Tallahassee. Every December lodge members gather to ring the bell for the
Salvation Army in downtown Jacksonville. Any member of the Jewish Community
interested in joining B’NAI B’RITH should contact the lodge president, Stuart
Hecht at 731-3406 for further information.
Stuart I. Hecht
